class="mergedtoprow"><th scope="row">Website</th><td>\n<span class="url"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.winnipeg.ca/interhom/">www<wbr />.winnipeg<wbr />.ca<wbr />/interhom<wbr />/</a></span></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Winnipeg</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/\xcb\x88/: primary stress follows">\xcb\x88</span><span title="&#39;w&#39; in &#39;wind&#39;">w</span><span title="/\xc9\xaa/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">\xc9\xaa</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/\xc9\xaa/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">\xc9\xaa</span><span title="&#39;p&#39; in &#39;pie&#39;">p</span><span title="/\xc9\x9b/: &#39;e&#39; in &#39;dress&#39;">\xc9\x9b</span><span title="/\xc9\xa1/: &#39;g&#39; in &#39;guy&#39;">\xc9\xa1</span></span>/</a></span><small class="nowrap">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Winnipeg.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c8/Winnipeg.ogg" class="internal" title="Winnipeg.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small></span>) is the capital and largest city of the province of <a href="/wiki/Manitoba" title="Manitoba">Manitoba</a> in <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a>. Centred on the <a href="/wiki/Confluence" title="Confluence">confluence</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Red_River_of_the_North" title="Red River of the North">Red</a> and <a href="/wiki/Assiniboine_River" title="Assiniboine River">Assiniboine</a> rivers, it is near the longitudinal centre of <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, approximately 110 kilometres (70&#160;mi) north of the <a href="/wiki/Canada%E2%80%93United_States_border" title="Canada\xe2\x80\x93United States border">Canada\xe2\x80\x93United States border</a>.\n</p><p>The city is named after the nearby <a href="/wiki/Lake_Winnipeg" title="Lake Winnipeg">Lake Winnipeg</a>; the name comes from the <a href="/wiki/Cree_language" title="Cree language">Western Cree</a> words for muddy water. The region was a trading centre for <a href="/wiki/Aboriginal_peoples_in_Canada" class="mw-redirect" title="Aboriginal peoples in Canada">aboriginal peoples</a> long before the arrival of Europeans. French traders built the first fort on the site in 1738. A settlement was later founded by the <a href="/wiki/Red_River_Colony" title="Red River Colony">Selkirk settlers</a> of the Red River Colony in 1812, the nucleus of which was incorporated as the City of Winnipeg in 1873. As of 2011, Winnipeg is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_the_100_largest_municipalities_in_Canada_by_population" title="List of the 100 largest municipalities in Canada by population">seventh most populated municipality</a> in Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-2006CensCSDPop_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2006CensCSDPop-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Being far inland, the <a href="/wiki/Winnipeg#Climate" title="Winnipeg">local climate</a> is extremely seasonal even by Canadian standards with average January lows of around \xe2\x88\x9221&#160;\xc2\xb0C (\xe2\x88\x926&#160;\xc2\xb0F) and average July highs of 26&#160;\xc2\xb0C (79&#160;\xc2\xb0F).<sup id="cite_ref-Environment_Canada_1981-2010_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Environment_Canada_1981-2010-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Known as the "Gateway to the West", Winnipeg is a railway and transportation hub with a diversified economy. This multicultural city hosts numerous annual festivals, including the <a href="/wiki/Festival_du_Voyageur" title="Festival du Voyageur">Festival du Voyageur</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Winnipeg_Folk_Festival" title="Winnipeg Folk Festival">Winnipeg Folk Festival</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Jazz_Winnipeg_Festival" title="Jazz Winnipeg Festival">Jazz Winnipeg Festival</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Winnipeg_Fringe_Theatre_Festival" title="Winnipeg Fringe Theatre Festival">Winnipeg Fringe Theatre Festival</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Folklorama" title="Folklorama">Folklorama</a>. Winnipeg was the first Canadian host of the <a href="/wiki/Pan_American_Games" title="Pan American Games">Pan American Games</a>. It is home to several professional sports franchises, including the <a href="/wiki/Winnipeg_Blue_Bombers" title="Winnipeg Blue Bombers">Winnipeg Blue Bombers</a> (<a href="/wiki/Canadian_football" title="Canadian football">Canadian football</a>), the <a href="/wiki/Winnipeg_Jets" title="Winnipeg Jets">Winnipeg Jets</a> (<a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey" title="Ice hockey">ice hockey</a>), <a href="/wiki/Manitoba_Moose" title="Manitoba Moose">Manitoba Moose</a> (ice hockey) and the <a href="/wiki/Winnipeg_Goldeyes" title="Winnipeg Goldeyes">Winnipeg Goldeyes</a> (<a href="/wiki/Baseball" title="Baseball">baseball</a>).\n</p>\n<div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" style="display:none" /><div class="toctitle" class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Winnipeg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Winnipeg" title="History of Winnipeg">History of Winnipeg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Winnipeg_history" title="Timeline of Winnipeg history">Timeline of Winnipeg history</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Winnipeg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Winnipeg lies at the <a href="/wiki/Confluence" title="Confluence">confluence</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Assiniboine_River" title="Assiniboine River">Assiniboine</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Red_River_Valley" title="Red River Valley">Red River of the North</a>, a location now known as "<a href="/wiki/The_Forks,_Winnipeg" title="The Forks, Winnipeg">The Forks</a>". This point was at the crossroads of <a href="/wiki/Canoe" title="Canoe">canoe</a> routes travelled by <a href="/wiki/First_Nations" title="First Nations">First Nations</a> before European contact.<sup id="cite_ref-forkshistory_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forkshistory-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> Winnipeg is named after nearby <a href="/wiki/Lake_Winnipeg" title="Lake Winnipeg">Lake Winnipeg</a>; the name is a transcription of the <a href="/wiki/Cree_language" title="Cree language">Western Cree</a> words for muddy or brackish water.<sup id="cite_ref-encbrit_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-encbrit-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ce_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ce-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Evidence provided by archaeology, <a href="/wiki/Petroglyph" title="Petroglyph">petroglyphs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rock_art" title="Rock art">rock art</a> and oral history indicates that native peoples used the area in prehistoric times for camping, harvesting, hunting, tool making, fishing, trading and, farther north, for agriculture.<sup id="cite_ref-harris_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harris-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Estimates of the date of first settlement in this area range from 11,500 years ago for a site southwest of the present city to 6,000 years ago at The Forks.<sup id="cite_ref-ce_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ce-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> In 1805, Canadian colonists observed First Nations peoples engaged in farming activity along the Red River. The practice quickly expanded, driven by the demand by traders for provisions.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The rivers provided an extensive transportation network linking northern First Peoples with those to the south along the <a href="/wiki/Missouri_River" title="Missouri River">Missouri</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mississippi_River" title="Mississippi River">Mississippi</a> rivers. The <a href="/wiki/Ojibwe" title="Ojibwe">Ojibwe</a> made some of the first maps on <a href="/wiki/Birch_bark" title="Birch bark">birch bark</a>, which helped <a href="/wiki/Fur_trade" title="Fur trade">fur traders</a> navigate the waterways of the area.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pierre_Gaultier_de_Varennes,_sieur_de_La_V%C3%A9rendrye" title="Pierre Gaultier de Varennes, sieur de La V\xc3\xa9rendrye">Sieur de La V\xc3\xa9rendrye</a> built the first fur <a href="/wiki/Trading_post" title="Trading post">trading post</a> on the site in 1738, called <a href="/wiki/Fort_Rouge_(fortification)" title="Fort Rouge (fortification)">Fort Rouge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> French trading continued at this site for several decades before the arrival of the British <a href="/wiki/Hudson%27s_Bay_Company" title="Hudson&#39;s Bay Company">Hudson\'s Bay Company</a> after France ceded the territory following its defeat in the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Years%27_War" title="Seven Years&#39; War">Seven Years\' War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Many French men who were trappers <a href="/wiki/Marriage_%27%C3%A0_la_fa%C3%A7on_du_pays%27" title="Marriage &#39;\xc3\xa0 la fa\xc3\xa7on du pays&#39;">married First Nations women</a>; their mixed-race children hunted, traded, and lived in the area. They gradually developed as an ethnicity known as the <a href="/wiki/M%C3%A9tis_people_(Canada)" class="mw-redirect" title="M\xc3\xa9tis people (Canada)">M\xc3\xa9tis</a> because of sharing a traditional culture.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rindisbacher_fishing_1821_large_(1).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Rindisbacher_fishing_1821_large_%281%29.png/220px-Rindisbacher_fishing_1821_large_%281%29.png" width="220" height="143" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Rindisbacher_fishing_1821_large_%281%29.png/330px-Rindisbacher_fishing_1821_large_%281%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/Rindisbacher_fishing_1821_large_%281%29.png/440px-Rindisbacher_fishing_1821_large_%281%29.png 2x" data-file-width="2388" data-file-height="1550" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rindisbacher_fishing_1821_large_(1).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An 1821 painting of winter fishing on the ice of the <a href="/wiki/Assiniboine_River" title="Assiniboine River">Assiniboine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Red_River_of_the_North" title="Red River of the North">Red</a> rivers. <a href="/wiki/Fort_Gibraltar" title="Fort Gibraltar">Fort Gibraltar</a> was erected in 1809.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Douglas,_5th_Earl_of_Selkirk" title="Thomas Douglas, 5th Earl of Selkirk">Lord Selkirk</a> was involved with the first permanent settlement (known as the <a href="/wiki/Red_River_Colony" title="Red River Colony">Red River Colony</a>), the purchase of land from the Hudson\'s Bay Company, and a survey of river lots in the early 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/North_West_Company" title="North West Company">North West Company</a> built <a href="/wiki/Fort_Gibraltar" title="Fort Gibraltar">Fort Gibraltar</a> in 1809, and the Hudson\'s Bay Company built <a href="/wiki/Fort_Douglas_(Canada)" title="Fort Douglas (Canada)">Fort Douglas</a> in 1812, both in the area of present-day Winnipeg.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> The two companies competed fiercely over trade.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> The M\xc3\xa9tis and Lord Selkirk\'s settlers fought at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Seven_Oaks" title="Battle of Seven Oaks">Battle of Seven Oaks</a> in 1816. In 1821, the Hudson\'s Bay and North West Companies merged, ending their long rivalry.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> Fort Gibraltar was renamed <a href="/wiki/Fort_Garry" title="Fort Garry">Fort Garry</a> in 1822 and became the leading post in the region for the Hudson\'s Bay Company.<sup id="cite_ref-timeline_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> A flood destroyed the fort in 1826 and it was not rebuilt until 1835.<sup id="cite_ref-timeline_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> A rebuilt section of the fort, consisting of the front gate and a section of the wall, is near the modern-day corner of Main Street and Broadway Avenue in downtown Winnipeg.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1869\xe2\x80\x9370, present-day Winnipeg was the site of the <a href="/wiki/Red_River_Rebellion" title="Red River Rebellion">Red River Rebellion</a>, a conflict between the local provisional government of M\xc3\xa9tis, led by <a href="/wiki/Louis_Riel" title="Louis Riel">Louis Riel</a>, and newcomers from eastern Canada. General <a href="/wiki/Garnet_Wolseley,_1st_Viscount_Wolseley" title="Garnet Wolseley, 1st Viscount Wolseley">Garnet Wolseley</a> was sent to put down the uprising. The <a href="/wiki/Manitoba_Act" title="Manitoba Act">Manitoba Act</a> of 1870 made Manitoba the <a href="/wiki/Provinces_and_territories_of_Canada" title="Provinces and territories of Canada">fifth province</a> of the three-year-old <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Confederation" title="Canadian Confederation">Canadian Confederation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Treaty_1" title="Treaty 1">Treaty 1</a>, which encompassed the city and much of the surrounding area, was signed on 3 August 1871 by representatives of the Crown and local Indigenous groups, comprising the Brokenhead Ojibway, Sagkeeng, Long Plain, Peguis, Roseau River Anishinabe, Sandy Bay and Swan Lake communities.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> On 8 November 1873, Winnipeg was incorporated as a city, with the Selkirk settlement as its nucleus.<sup id="cite_ref-Winnipeg&#39;s_History_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Winnipeg&#39;s_History-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> M\xc3\xa9tis legislator and interpreter <a href="/wiki/James_McKay_(fur_trader)" title="James McKay (fur trader)">James McKay</a> named the city.<sup id="cite_ref-mckaysidea_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mckaysidea-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Winnipeg\'s mandate was to govern and provide municipal services to citizens attracted to trade expansion between <a href="/wiki/Fort_Garry" title="Fort Garry">Upper Fort Garry</a> / <a href="/wiki/Lower_Fort_Garry" title="Lower Fort Garry">Lower Fort Garry</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saint_Paul,_Minnesota" title="Saint Paul, Minnesota">Saint Paul, Minnesota</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Winnipeg developed rapidly after the coming of the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Pacific_Railway" title="Canadian Pacific Railway">Canadian Pacific Railway</a> in 1881.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> The railway divided the North End, which housed mainly Eastern Europeans, from the richer Anglo-Saxon southern part of the city.<sup id="cite_ref-ce_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ce-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> It also contributed to a demographic shift beginning shortly after Confederation that saw the francophone population decrease from a majority to a small minority group. This shift resulted in Premier <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Greenway" title="Thomas Greenway">Thomas Greenway</a> controversially ending legislative bilingualism and <a href="/wiki/Manitoba_Schools_Question" title="Manitoba Schools Question">removing funding for French Catholic Schools</a> in 1890.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Modern_history_.281900.E2.80.93present.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_history_(1900\xe2\x80\x93present)">Modern history (1900\xe2\x80\x93present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Winnipeg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Modern history (1900\xe2\x80\x93present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>By 1911, Winnipeg was Canada\'s third-largest city.<sup id="cite_ref-ce_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ce-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> However, the city faced financial difficulty when the <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal" title="Panama Canal">Panama Canal</a> opened in 1914.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The canal reduced reliance on Canada\'s rail system for international trade; the increase in shipping traffic helped <a href="/wiki/Vancouver" title="Vancouver">Vancouver</a> to surpass Winnipeg in both prosperity and population by the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WinnipegGeneralStrike.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/WinnipegGeneralStrike.jpg/220px-WinnipegGeneralStrike.jpg" width="220" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/WinnipegGeneralStrike.jpg/330px-WinnipegGeneralStrike.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/WinnipegGeneralStrike.jpg/440px-WinnipegGeneralStrike.jpg 2x" data-file-width="595" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WinnipegGeneralStrike.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Crowd gathered outside old City Hall during the <a href="/wiki/Winnipeg_General_Strike" class="mw-redirect" title="Winnipeg General Strike">Winnipeg General Strike</a> in 1919.</div></div></div>\n<p>More than 30,000 workers walked off their jobs in May 1919 in what came to be known as the <a href="/wiki/Winnipeg_general_strike" title="Winnipeg general strike">Winnipeg general strike</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The strike was a product of postwar recession, labour conditions, the activity of union organizers and a large influx of returning World War I soldiers seeking work.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> After many arrests, deportations, and incidents of violence, the strike ended on 21 June 1919 when the <a href="/wiki/Riot_Act" title="Riot Act">Riot Act</a> was read and a group of <a href="/wiki/Royal_Canadian_Mounted_Police" title="Royal Canadian Mounted Police">Royal Canadian Mounted Police</a> officers charged a group of strikers.<sup id="cite_ref-bloody_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloody-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Two strikers were killed and at least thirty others were injured on the day that became known as <i>Bloody Saturday</i>; the event polarized the population.<sup id="cite_ref-bloody_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bloody-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> One of the leaders of the strike, <a href="/wiki/J._S._Woodsworth" title="J. S. Woodsworth">J. S. Woodsworth</a>, went on to found Canada\'s first major socialist party, the <a href="/wiki/Co-operative_Commonwealth_Federation" title="Co-operative Commonwealth Federation">Co-operative Commonwealth Federation</a>, which later became the <a href="/wiki/New_Democratic_Party_(Canada)" class="mw-redirect" title="New Democratic Party (Canada)">New Democratic Party</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Manitoba_Legislative_Building" title="Manitoba Legislative Building">Manitoba Legislative Building</a>, constructed mainly of <a href="/wiki/Tyndall_stone" title="Tyndall stone">Tyndall stone</a>, opened in 1920; its dome supports a bronze statue finished in gold leaf, titled "Eternal Youth and the Spirit of Enterprise" (commonly known as the "<a href="/wiki/Golden_Boy_(Manitoba)" title="Golden Boy (Manitoba)">Golden Boy</a>").<sup id="cite_ref-History_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-History-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Stock_market_crash_of_1929" class="mw-redirect" title="Stock market crash of 1929">stock market crash of 1929</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> resulted in widespread unemployment, worsened by drought and low agricultural prices.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The Depression ended after the start of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> in 1939.<sup id="cite_ref-ce_17-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ce-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> \n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Wfp_1942.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/10/Wfp_1942.jpg/220px-Wfp_1942.jpg" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/10/Wfp_1942.jpg/330px-Wfp_1942.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/10/Wfp_1942.jpg/440px-Wfp_1942.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="330" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Wfp_1942.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>In 1942, the Canadian Victory Loan campaign <a href="/wiki/If_Day" title="If Day">simulated</a> a <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi</a> occupation of the city to raise <a href="/wiki/War_bonds" class="mw-redirect" title="War bonds">war bonds</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hong_Kong" title="Battle of Hong Kong">Battle of Hong Kong</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Winnipeg_Grenadiers" title="The Winnipeg Grenadiers">The Winnipeg Grenadiers</a> were among the first Canadians to engage in combat against Japan. Battalion members who survived combat were taken prisoner and endured brutal treatment in <a href="/wiki/Prisoner_of_war" title="Prisoner of war">prisoner of war</a> camps.<sup id="cite_ref-Vance1994_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Vance1994-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> In 1942, the Victory Loan Campaign staged a <a href="/wiki/If_Day" title="If Day">mock Nazi invasion</a> of Winnipeg to promote awareness of the stakes of the war in Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> When the war ended, pent-up demand generated a boom in housing development, although building activity was checked by the <a href="/wiki/1950_Red_River_flood" title="1950 Red River flood">1950 Red River flood</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-flood_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-flood-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The federal government estimated damage at over $26 million, although the province indicated that it was at least double that.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Prior to 1972, Winnipeg was the largest of thirteen cities and towns in a metropolitan area around the Red and Assiniboine Rivers. In 1960 the Metropolitan Corporation of Greater Winnipeg was established to co-ordinate service delivery in the metropolitan region.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> A consolidated metropolitan "<a href="/wiki/Amalgamation_of_Winnipeg" title="Amalgamation of Winnipeg">unicity</a>" government incorporating Winnipeg and its surrounding municipalities was established on 27 July 1971, taking effect in 1972.<sup id="cite_ref-unicity_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-unicity-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/City_of_Winnipeg_Act" class="mw-redirect" title="City of Winnipeg Act">City of Winnipeg Act</a> incorporated the current city.<sup id="cite_ref-ce_17-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ce-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> In 2003 the City of Winnipeg Act was repealed and replaced with the City of Winnipeg Charter.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Winnipeg experienced a severe economic downturn in advance of the <a href="/wiki/Early_1980s_recession" title="Early 1980s recession">early 1980s recession</a>, during which the city incurred closures of prominent businesses, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Winnipeg_Tribune" title="Winnipeg Tribune">Winnipeg Tribune</a></i>, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Swift_%26_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Swift &amp; Company">Swift\'s</a> and <a href="/wiki/Canada_Packers" class="mw-redirect" title="Canada Packers">Canada Packers</a> meat packing plants.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> In 1981, Winnipeg was one of the first cities in Canada to sign a tripartite agreement with the provincial and federal governments to redevelop its downtown area,<sup id="cite_ref-SanctonYoung2009_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SanctonYoung2009-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> and the three levels of government contributed over $271 million to its development.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> In 1989, the reclamation and redevelopment of the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_National_Railway" title="Canadian National Railway">CNR</a> <a href="/wiki/Rail_yard" title="Rail yard">rail yards</a> turned The Forks into Winnipeg\'s most popular tourist attraction.<sup id="cite_ref-forkshistory_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forkshistory-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ce_17-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ce-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> The city was threatened by the <a href="/wiki/1997_Red_River_flood" title="1997 Red River flood">1997 Red River flood</a> as well as further floods in <a href="/wiki/2009_Red_River_flood" title="2009 Red River flood">2009</a> and <a href="/wiki/2011_Red_River_flood" title="2011 Red River flood">2011</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> in each of these floods, the <a href="/wiki/Red_River_Floodway" title="Red River Floodway">Red River Floodway</a> was used to safely protect the city.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Winnipeg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_and_climate_of_Winnipeg" title="Geography and climate of Winnipeg">Geography and climate of Winnipeg</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:2005_Winnipeg_Flood.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/2005_Winnipeg_Flood.JPG/220px-2005_Winnipeg_Flood.JPG" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/2005_Winnipeg_Flood.JPG/330px-2005_Winnipeg_Flood.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/2005_Winnipeg_Flood.JPG/440px-2005_Winnipeg_Flood.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1632" data-file-height="1232" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:2005_Winnipeg_Flood.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Docks at <a href="/wiki/The_Forks,_Winnipeg" title="The Forks, Winnipeg">The Forks</a>. The city lies at the bottom of the <a href="/wiki/Red_River_Valley" title="Red River Valley">Red River Valley</a>, a flood plain with a flat topography.</div></div></div>\n<p>Winnipeg lies at the bottom of the <a href="/wiki/Red_River_Valley" title="Red River Valley">Red River Valley</a>, a flood plain with an extremely flat topography.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> It is on the eastern edge of the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Prairies" title="Canadian Prairies">Canadian Prairies</a> in <a href="/wiki/Western_Canada" title="Western Canada">Western Canada</a> and is known as the "Gateway to the West".<sup id="cite_ref-ce_17-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ce-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> Winnipeg is bordered by <a href="/wiki/Tallgrass_prairie" title="Tallgrass prairie">tallgrass prairie</a> to the west and south and the <a href="/wiki/Aspen_parkland" title="Aspen parkland">aspen parkland</a> to the northeast, although most of the native prairie grasses have been removed for agriculture and urbanization.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> It is relatively close to many large <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Shield" title="Canadian Shield">Canadian Shield</a> lakes and parks, as well as <a href="/wiki/Lake_Winnipeg" title="Lake Winnipeg">Lake Winnipeg</a> (<a href="/wiki/List_of_lakes_by_area" title="List of lakes by area">the Earth\'s 11th largest freshwater lake</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Winnipeg has North America\'s largest extant mature urban elm forest.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> The city has an area of 464.08&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (179.18&#160;sq&#160;mi).<sup id="cite_ref-area_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-area-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Winnipeg has four major rivers: the <a href="/wiki/Red_River_of_the_North" title="Red River of the North">Red</a>, <a href="/wiki/Assiniboine_River" title="Assiniboine River">Assiniboine</a>, <a href="/wiki/La_Salle_River" title="La Salle River">La Salle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seine_River_(Manitoba)" title="Seine River (Manitoba)">Seine</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> The city was subject to severe flooding in the past. The Red River reached its greatest flood height in <a href="/wiki/1826_Red_River_flood" title="1826 Red River flood">1826</a>. Another large flood in <a href="/wiki/1950_Red_River_flood" title="1950 Red River flood">1950</a> caused millions of dollars in damage and mass evacuations.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> This flood prompted <a href="/wiki/Dufferin_Roblin" title="Dufferin Roblin">Duff Roblin</a>\'s provincial government to build the <a href="/wiki/Red_River_Floodway" title="Red River Floodway">Red River Floodway</a> to protect the city; the project began in 1962 and was completed in 1968.<sup id="cite_ref-ce_17-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ce-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/1997_Red_River_flood" title="1997 Red River flood">1997 flood</a>, flood control dikes were reinforced and raised using sandbags; Winnipeg suffered limited damage compared to the flood\'s impact on cities without such structures, such as <a href="/wiki/Grand_Forks,_North_Dakota" title="Grand Forks, North Dakota">Grand Forks, North Dakota</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> The generally flat terrain and the poor drainage of the Red River Valley\'s clay-based soil also results in many <a href="/wiki/Mosquito" title="Mosquito">mosquitoes</a> during wetter years.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Winnipeg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:River_River_Curling.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/River_River_Curling.jpg/220px-River_River_Curling.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/River_River_Curling.jpg/330px-River_River_Curling.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/River_River_Curling.jpg/440px-River_River_Curling.jpg 2x" data-file-width="980" data-file-height="652" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:River_River_Curling.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Winters are the coldest and driest time of the year in Winnipeg.</div></div></div>\n<p>Winnipeg\'s location in the Canadian Prairies gives it a <a href="/wiki/Humid_continental_climate" title="Humid continental climate">humid continental climate</a><sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="K\xc3\xb6ppen climate classification">K\xc3\xb6ppen</a> <i>Dfb</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> with warm, humid summers, and long, cold winters. Summers have a July mean average of 19.7&#160;\xc2\xb0C (67.5&#160;\xc2\xb0F).<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Winters are the coldest and driest time of year, with the January mean average around \xe2\x88\x9216.4&#160;\xc2\xb0C (2.5&#160;\xc2\xb0F) and total winter precipitation averaging 55&#160;mm (2.2&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> Temperatures occasionally drop below \xe2\x88\x9240.0&#160;\xc2\xb0C (\xe2\x88\x9240&#160;\xc2\xb0F).<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> The actual air temperature drops below \xe2\x88\x9230&#160;\xc2\xb0C (\xe2\x88\x9222&#160;\xc2\xb0F) on average 12.6 days annually<sup id="cite_ref-Environment_Canada_1981-2010_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Environment_Canada_1981-2010-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> and rises above 30&#160;\xc2\xb0C (86&#160;\xc2\xb0F) 13.3 days of the year.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> On average there are 317.8 days per year with measurable sunshine, with July seeing the most on average.<sup id="cite_ref-Canadian_Climate_Normals_1971-2000_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canadian_Climate_Normals_1971-2000-73">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> With 2353 hours of sunshine per year, Winnipeg is the second sunniest city in Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> Total annual precipitation (both rain and snow) is just over 51 centimetres (20&#160;in).<sup id="cite_ref-ce/ref_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ce/ref-75">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> Thunderstorms are very common during summer, and sometimes severe enough to produce tornadoes.<sup id="cite_ref-storms_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-storms-76">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Low <a href="/wiki/Wind_chill" title="Wind chill">wind chill</a> values are a common occurrence in the local climate. The wind chill has gone down as low as \xe2\x88\x9257&#160;\xc2\xb0C (\xe2\x88\x9270.6&#160;\xc2\xb0F) and on average there are twelve days of the year that can reach a wind chill below \xe2\x88\x9240&#160;\xc2\xb0C (\xe2\x88\x9240&#160;\xc2\xb0F).<sup id="cite_ref-Environment_Canada_1981-2010_7-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Environment_Canada_1981-2010-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The highest temperature ever recorded in Winnipeg was 42.2&#160;\xc2\xb0C (108&#160;\xc2\xb0F) on 11 July 1936 while the highest daily low temperature was 28.3&#160;\xc2\xb0C (82.9&#160;\xc2\xb0F) on 12 July 1936.<sup id="cite_ref-July_1936_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-July_1936-77">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Apparent_temperature" title="Apparent temperature">apparent heat</a> can be even more extreme due to bursts of humidity, and on 25 July 2007 a <a href="/wiki/Humidex" title="Humidex">humidex</a> reading of 47.3&#160;\xc2\xb0C (117.1&#160;\xc2\xb0F) was measured.<sup id="cite_ref-Environment_Canada_1981-2010_7-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Environment_Canada_1981-2010-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The coldest temperature ever recorded was \xe2\x88\x9247.8&#160;\xc2\xb0C (\xe2\x88\x9254.0&#160;\xc2\xb0F) on 24 December 1879.<sup id="cite_ref-December_1879_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-December_1879-78">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The frost-free season is comparatively long for a location with such severe winters. The last spring frost is on average around 23 May, whilst the first fall frost is on 22 September.<sup id="cite_ref-Environment_Canada_1981-2010_7-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Environment_Canada_1981-2010-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable collapsible collapsed" style="width:90%; text-align:center; font-size:90%; line-height: 1.2em; class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Winnipeg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Cityscape">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Winnipeg_neighbourhoods" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Winnipeg neighbourhoods">List of Winnipeg neighbourhoods</a>, <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_in_Winnipeg" title="List of tallest buildings in Winnipeg">List of tallest buildings in Winnipeg</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Subdivisions_of_Winnipeg" title="Subdivisions of Winnipeg">Subdivisions of Winnipeg</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Downtown_Area,_Winnipeg_-_panoramio_(1).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Downtown_Area%2C_Winnipeg_-_panoramio_%281%29.jpg/220px-Downtown_Area%2C_Winnipeg_-_panoramio_%281%29.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Downtown_Area%2C_Winnipeg_-_panoramio_%281%29.jpg/330px-Downtown_Area%2C_Winnipeg_-_panoramio_%281%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Downtown_Area%2C_Winnipeg_-_panoramio_%281%29.jpg/440px-Downtown_Area%2C_Winnipeg_-_panoramio_%281%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3532" data-file-height="2352" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Downtown_Area,_Winnipeg_-_panoramio_(1).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Centred on the intersection of <a href="/wiki/Portage_and_Main" title="Portage and Main">Portage and Main</a>, <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Winnipeg" title="Downtown Winnipeg">Downtown Winnipeg</a> is the city\'s <a href="/wiki/Central_business_district" title="Central business district">central business district</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>There are officially 236 neighbourhoods in Winnipeg.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Winnipeg" title="Downtown Winnipeg">Downtown Winnipeg</a>, the city\'s financial heart and economic core, is centred on the intersection of <a href="/wiki/Portage_and_Main" title="Portage and Main">Portage Avenue and Main Street</a>. It covers about 1 square mile (2.6&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) and is the city\'s fastest growing high-income neighbourhood.<sup id="cite_ref-BIZ_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BIZ-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> More than 72,000 people work downtown, and over 40,000 students attend classes at its universities and colleges.<sup id="cite_ref-BIZ_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BIZ-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The past few decades have seen the downtown undergo major revitalization efforts; since 1999, over <a href="/wiki/C$" class="mw-redirect" title="C$">C$</a>1.2 billion has been invested.<sup id="cite_ref-BIZ_84-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BIZ-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Downtown Winnipeg\'s <a href="/wiki/Exchange_District" title="Exchange District">Exchange District</a> is named after the area\'s original grain exchange, which operated from 1880 to 1913.<sup id="cite_ref-BIZ_84-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BIZ-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The 30-block district received <a href="/wiki/National_Historic_Sites_of_Canada" title="National Historic Sites of Canada">National Historic Site of Canada</a> status in 1997; it includes North America\'s most extensive collection of early 20th-century <a href="/wiki/Terracotta" title="Terracotta">terracotta</a> and cut stone architecture, 62 of downtown Winnipeg\'s 86 heritage structures,<sup id="cite_ref-BIZ_84-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BIZ-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Juba" title="Stephen Juba">Stephen Juba</a> Park, and Old Market Square.<sup id="cite_ref-BIZ_84-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BIZ-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Other major downtown areas are <a href="/wiki/The_Forks,_Winnipeg" title="The Forks, Winnipeg">The Forks</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Park_(Winnipeg)" title="Central Park (Winnipeg)">Central Park</a>, Broadway-Assiniboine and <a href="/wiki/Chinatown,_Winnipeg" title="Chinatown, Winnipeg">Chinatown</a>. Many of Downtown Winnipeg\'s major buildings are linked with the <a href="/wiki/Winnipeg_Walkway" title="Winnipeg Walkway">Winnipeg Walkway</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Residential neighbourhoods surround the downtown in all directions; expansion is greatest to the south and west, although several areas remain underdeveloped.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> The city\'s largest park, <a href="/wiki/Assiniboine_Park" title="Assiniboine Park">Assiniboine Park</a>, houses the <a href="/wiki/Assiniboine_Park_Zoo" title="Assiniboine Park Zoo">Assiniboine Park Zoo</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Leo_Mol" title="Leo Mol">Leo Mol Sculpture Garden</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> Other large city parks include <a href="/wiki/Kildonan_Park" title="Kildonan Park">Kildonan Park</a> and <a href="/wiki/St._Vital_Park" title="St. Vital Park">St. Vital Park</a>. The city\'s major commercial areas are <a href="/wiki/Polo_Park" title="Polo Park">Polo Park</a>, Kildonan Crossing, South St. Vital, Garden City (West Kildonan), Pembina Strip, Kenaston Smart Centre, <a href="/wiki/Osborne_Village" title="Osborne Village">Osborne Village</a>, and the Corydon strip.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> The main cultural and nightlife areas are the Exchange District, The Forks, Osborne Village and Corydon Village (both in Fort Rouge), Sargent and Ellice Avenues (West End) and Old St. Boniface.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Osborne_Village" title="Osborne Village">Osborne Village</a> is Winnipeg\'s most densely populated neighbourhood<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> and one of the most densely populated neighbourhoods in Western Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Winnipeg&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Winnipeg" title="Demographics of Winnipeg">Demographics of Winnipeg</a></div>\n<table class="toccolours collapsible collapsed" style="width:30em;border-spacing: 0;float:right;clear:right;margin:0.5em 0 1em 0.5em;"><tbody><tr><th href="#cite_note-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>As of the <a href="/wiki/Canada_2016_Census" class="mw-redirect" title="Canada 2016 Census">Canada 2016 Census</a> there were 705,244 people living in Winnipeg proper,<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> with approximately 778,489 living in the Winnipeg Census Metropolitan Area (CMA).<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Thus, Winnipeg is Manitoba\'s largest city and Canada\'s seventh largest city. Furthermore, the city represents 54.9% of the population of the province of Manitoba, the highest population concentration in one city of any province in Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Apart from the city of Winnipeg, the Winnipeg CMA includes the <a href="/wiki/Rural_municipality" title="Rural municipality">rural municipalities</a> of <a href="/wiki/Rural_Municipality_of_Springfield" title="Rural Municipality of Springfield">Springfield</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rural_Municipality_of_St._Clements" title="Rural Municipality of St. Clements">St. Clements</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rural_Municipality_of_Tach%C3%A9" title="Rural Municipality of Tach\xc3\xa9">Tach\xc3\xa9</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rural_Municipality_of_East_St._Paul" title="Rural Municipality of East St. Paul">East St. Paul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rural_Municipality_of_Macdonald" title="Rural Municipality of Macdonald">Macdonald</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rural_Municipality_of_Ritchot" title="Rural Municipality of Ritchot">Ritchot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rural_Municipality_of_West_St._Paul" title="Rural Municipality of West St. Paul">West St. Paul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rural_Municipality_of_Headingley" title="Rural Municipality of Headingley">Headingley</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Brokenhead_4,_Manitoba" class="mw-redirect" title="Brokenhead 4, Manitoba">Brokenhead 4 reserve</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rural_Municipality_of_Rosser" title="Rural Municipality of Rosser">Rosser</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rural_Municipality_of_St._Fran%C3%A7ois_Xavier" title="Rural Municipality of St. Fran\xc3\xa7ois Xavier">St. Fran\xc3\xa7ois Xavier</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Statistics_Canada" title="Statistics Canada">Statistics Canada</a>\'s estimate of the Winnipeg CMA population as of 1 July 2017 is 825,713, making it the 7th largest CMA in Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table cellpadding="1" style="float:left; margin:0 1em 1em 0; border:1px #bbb solid; border-collapse:collapse; font-size:70%; background:#eee;">\n<td>Scottish\n</td>\n<td>113,465\n</td>\n<td>17.4\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td>Canadian\n</td>\n<td>108,955\n</td>\n<td>16.76\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center; background:#eee;">\n<td>German\n</td>\n<td>105,910\n</td>\n<td>16.2\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td>Ukrainian\n</td>\n<td>98,860\n</td>\n<td>15.2\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center; background:#eee;">\n<td>Irish\n</td>\n<td>85,800\n</td>\n<td>13.2\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td>French\n</td>\n<td>85,025\n</td>\n<td>13.1\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center; background:#eee;">\n<td>Aboriginal (incl. M\xc3\xa9tis)\n</td>\n<td>76,055\n</td>\n<td>11.7\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center;">\n<td>Filipino\n</td>\n<td>58,255\n</td>\n<td>9.0\n</td></tr>\n<tr style="text-align:center; background:#eee;">\n<td>Polish\n</td>\n<td>50,385\n</td>\n<td>7.8\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>As of the 2006 census, 48.3 percent of residents were male and 51.7 percent were female. 24.3 percent were 19 years old or younger, 27.4 percent were between 20 and 30 years old, and 34.0 percent were between 40 and 64 years old. The average age of a Winnipegger in May 2006 was 38.7, compared to an average of 39.5 for Canada as a whole.<sup id="cite_ref-2006CityProfile_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2006CityProfile-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Between the censuses of 2006 and 2011, Winnipeg\'s population increased by 4.8 percent, compared to 5.2 percent for Manitoba as a whole. The population density of the city of Winnipeg averaged 1,430 people per km<sup>2</sup>, compared with 2.2 for Manitoba.<sup id="cite_ref-cy11_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cy11-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ST._BONIFACE_CATHEDRAL_05.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/ST._BONIFACE_CATHEDRAL_05.jpg/220px-ST._BONIFACE_CATHEDRAL_05.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/ST._BONIFACE_CATHEDRAL_05.jpg/330px-ST._BONIFACE_CATHEDRAL_05.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/ST._BONIFACE_CATHEDRAL_05.jpg/440px-ST._BONIFACE_CATHEDRAL_05.jpg 2x" data-file-width="5442" data-file-height="3628" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ST._BONIFACE_CATHEDRAL_05.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Saint_Boniface_Cathedral" title="Saint Boniface Cathedral">Saint Boniface Cathedral</a> is the seat for the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Archdiocese_of_Saint_Boniface" title="Roman Catholic Archdiocese of Saint Boniface">Roman Catholic Archdiocese of Saint Boniface</a>. Catholicism is the largest <a href="/wiki/Christian_denomination" title="Christian denomination">Christian denomination</a> in the city.</div></div></div>\n<p>Winnipeg has a significant and increasing <a href="/wiki/Aboriginal_peoples_in_Canada" class="mw-redirect" title="Aboriginal peoples in Canada">Aboriginal</a> population, with both the highest percentage of Aboriginal peoples (12.5%) for any major Canadian city, and the highest total number of Aboriginals (86,035) for any single non-reserve municipality.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> The Aboriginal population grew by 22% between 2001 and 2006, compared to an increase of 3% for the city as a whole; this population tends to be younger and less wealthy than non-Aboriginal residents.<sup id="cite_ref-uaps_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uaps-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> Tension between Aboriginal groups and non-aboriginal groups has been growing in the city.<sup id="cite_ref-racism_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-racism-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Winnipeg also has the highest M\xc3\xa9tis population in both percentage (6.3%) and numbers (41,005); the growth rate for this population between 2001 and 2006 was 30%.<sup id="cite_ref-nhs11_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nhs11-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-uaps_110-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-uaps-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The city has the greatest percentage of Filipino residents (8.7%) of any major Canadian city, although <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a> has more Filipinos by total population. In 2006, Winnipeg ranked seventh of the Canadian cities for percentage of residents of a visible minority.<sup id="cite_ref-nhs11_106-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nhs11-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> As of the 2016 Census, the population was 63.9% European in origin (73.5% of the city was white in 2006), while non-aboriginal visible minorities represent 23.5% (up from 16.3% in 2006).<sup id="cite_ref-nhs11_106-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nhs11-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2006CityProfile_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2006CityProfile-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> The city receives over 10,000 net international immigrants per year.<sup id="cite_ref-ctr_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ctr-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>More than a hundred languages are spoken in Winnipeg, of which the most common is English: 99 percent of Winnipeggers are <a href="/wiki/Fluency" title="Fluency">fluent</a> English speakers, 88 percent speak only English, and 0.1 percent speak only French (Canada\'s other <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a>). 10 percent speak both English and French, while 1.3 percent speak neither. Other languages spoken as a <a href="/wiki/Mother_tongue" class="mw-redirect" title="Mother tongue">mother tongue</a> in Winnipeg include <a href="/wiki/Filipino_language" title="Filipino language">Tagalog</a> (5.0%), German (2.5%), and <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">Punjabi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_language" title="Ukrainian language">Ukrainian</a> (both 1.4%). Several Aboriginal languages are also spoken, such as <a href="/wiki/Ojibwe_language" title="Ojibwe language">Ojibwe</a> (0.3%) and <a href="/wiki/Cree_language" title="Cree language">Cree</a> (0.2%).<sup id="cite_ref-cy11_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cy11-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 2011 National Household Survey reported the religious make-up of Winnipeg as: 63.7% <a href="/wiki/Christian" title="Christian">Christian</a>, including 29.7% <a href="/wiki/Catholic" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic">Catholic</a>, 8.1% <a href="/wiki/United_Church_of_Canada" title="United Church of Canada">United Church</a>, and 4.6% <a href="/wiki/Anglican" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglican">Anglican</a>; 1.7% <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a>; 1.6% <a href="/wiki/Jewish" class="mw-redirect" title="Jewish">Jewish</a>; 1.5% <a href="/wiki/Sikh" title="Sikh">Sikh</a>; 1.0% <a href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a>; 1.0% <a href="/wiki/Buddhist" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a>; 0.3% <a href="/wiki/Native_American_religion" title="Native American religion">traditional (aboriginal) spirituality</a>; 0.4% other; and 28.9% no religious affiliation.<sup id="cite_ref-nhs11_106-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nhs11-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n\n<tbody><tr>\n<th colspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Ethnic_group" title="Ethnic group">Ethnic groups</a> in Winnipeg City (2016)<br /><span style="font-size:85%;">Source: <a
